<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b4ec893b-3882-43a6-ae7a-0acd4deccf05'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer13079621503116995222"><i>Once</i> by Morris Gleitzman is the story of a young Jewish boy who is determined to escape the orphanage he lives in to save his Jewish parents from the Nazis in the occupied Poland of the Second World War.<br/><br/><b>Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least Once.</b><br/><b></b><br/>Once I escaped from an orphanage to find Mum and Dad.<br/><br/>Once I saved a girl called Zelda from a burning </span>
  <span id="freeText13079621503116995222" style="display:none"><i>Once</i> by Morris Gleitzman is the story of a young Jewish boy who is determined to escape the orphanage he lives in to save his Jewish parents from the Nazis in the occupied Poland of the Second World War.<br /><br /><b>Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least Once.</b><br /><b></b><br />Once I escaped from an orphanage to find Mum and Dad.<br /><br />Once I saved a girl called Zelda from a burning house.<br /><br />Once I made a Nazi with a toothache laugh.<br /><br />My name is Felix. This is my story.<br /><br /><i>Once</i> is the first in a series of children's novels about Felix, a Jewish orphan caught in the middle of the Holocaust, from Australian author Morris Gleitzman - author of <i>Bumface</i> and <i>Boy Overboard</i>. The next books in the series <i>Then</i>, <i>Now</i> and <i>After</i> are also available from Puffin.</span>

<div id="review_158469516" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158469516" />
    <a title="Lyndsey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4224396-lyndsey"><img alt="Lyndsey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1288890060p2/4224396.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/158469516?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyndsey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyndsey" href="/user/show/4224396-lyndsey">Lyndsey</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780805090260" href="/book/show/7061392-once">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4224396-lyndsey?shelf=reviewed-2011">reviewed-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4224396-lyndsey?shelf=pov-primarily-male">pov-primarily-male</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4224396-lyndsey?shelf=reviewed-4-stars">reviewed-4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4224396-lyndsey?shelf=1st-person">1st-person</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer158469516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3527758646706333937">When I was young 'un, we had this storytelling board game in our house. If memory serves me right, it was called, simply, "Once..."<br/><br/><br/><br/>The basis of the game was to create a story from a card prompt and people had to guess whether it was true or not - or something like that anyway.<br/><br/><br/><br/><strong>As many things do, at first this game went over my head a bit.</strong> *swoosh* Because in my everyday life, whenever I would try to make up a story (or more accurately - what you might call a white lie) to my friends or family, th</span>
  <span id="freeText3527758646706333937" style="display:none">When I was young 'un, we had this storytelling board game in our house. If memory serves me right, it was called, simply, "Once..."<br /><br /><br /><br />The basis of the game was to create a story from a card prompt and people had to guess whether it was true or not - or something like that anyway.<br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>As many things do, at first this game went over my head a bit.</strong> *swoosh* Because in my everyday life, whenever I would try to make up a story (or more accurately - what you might call a white lie) to my friends or family, they always seemed to know right away. At least my mom, who I had convinced myself was secretly a CIA agent, always knew.<br /><br /><br /><br />But I eventually figured out that when it is turned into a game, this "storytelling" phenomenon can actually be quite fun, because apparently if you are being upfront about this thing called "fiction" being "not real", then it is perfectly acceptable and not something you have to be nervous about. Around that time, I started to get very good at it.<br /><br /><br /><br />So anyway, I played this game with my friends, and at one point, I told a gruesome story about how, just the previous week, I had seen a cat torture and kill a baby bird, basically skinning it alive. A fairly traumatic thing for a young girl to witness. Of course, everyone assumed that I had actually seen it happen, because what kid could just make something like that up? Well apparently, a kid with a sick mind. <em>I had made it up.</em><br /><br /><br /><br />With an incredibly active and visual imagination, not to mention, a little bit of craziness thrown in for good measure, <u>I had actually seen this scene happen solely in my mind's eye</u>. So, I didn't technically just make up the story on the fly; rather, as a result seeing a smushed dead baby bird lying on the street, my mind went on to imagine how this might have happened. Thus, I received the previously stated disturbing mental image.<br /><br /><br /><br />I am about 99.98646362728% certain that most people's minds don't work this way. I think maybe you need to be at least a little bit of a paranoid schizophrenic to be a storyteller. At least, that's how I explain my strange mental processes to myself.<br /><br /><br /><br />________________________________<br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>This book, entitled Once, is about a young Jewish boy, named Felix, living and suffering through the horrid times of the Holocaust.</strong> He has been hidden away in an orphanage and has convinced himself that his parents are still around and just waiting for the right moment to come and get him. When they don't show up after three years, Felix sets out to find them and meets new friends and new <em>trouble</em>.<br /><br /><br /><br /><u>The little boy loves to tell stories and does so throughout almost the entire book.</u> He isn't what you might think of as an "unreliable narrator", however. He is always upfront with the reader, so though he may tell "stories" to the other characters, he never does with us. A true unreliable narrator is more like what you would find in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5759.Fight_Club" title="Fight Club by Chuck Palahniuk" rel="nofollow noopener">Fight Club</a>, where the reader isn't given all the necessary information and much is withheld.<br /><br /><br /><br />The things that happen to and around Felix in this book are terrifyingly unbelievable. It is easy to tell yourself that things like this could never happen. But they have. They are. They will.<br /><br /><br /><br />The prose is simple and heart-wrenching. The writing is in no way strenuous, but the story is. Worry and heartache will easily overcome you in this book. It should be difficult to believe that human beings are capable of such atrocities but somehow, it isn't that difficult to imagine.<br /><br /><br /><br />We should be able to look at a book like this and say "No one would ever be capable of doing <strong>that</strong>." <em>But we can't.</em><br /><br /><br /><br />Overall, this was an excellent story about a young boy suffering through a horrible time in history. It certainly pulls at your heart strings, but I wouldn't quite call it life changing. <strong>This book comes off more like a deeply and darkly intense fable.</strong><br /><br /><br /><br /><u>The books is a short 163 pages</u>, and the final chapter leaves it somewhat open-ended. Just enough so, that it you leaves you asking questions but still gives you a bit of closure. Happy-endings are not readily available within Holocaust fiction, unfortunately.<br /><br /><br /><br />This is kind of book that resonates. It stays with you and keeps your mind working long after you have finished.<br /><br /><br /><br />If you are interested, the hardcover is currently the cheapest way to obtain this book <a target="_blank" href="http://amzn.to/gkU4ol" rel="nofollow noopener">at just under 7 dollars on Amazon</a>. The Kindle edition is very pricey at 10 dollars, especially for such a short book.<br /><br /><br /><br />The thing I loved most about this book is that throughout most of it, <u>Felix has an uplifting and optimistic personality</u>. It was so entertaining as well as endearing to hear his thoughts and views on the world. He is an amazing character and a new favorite of mine. His viewpoint reminded me ever so slightly of the main character in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Curious%20Incident%20of%20the%20Dog%20in%20the%20Nighttime" title="The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Nighttime" rel="nofollow noopener">The Curious Incident of the Dog in the Nighttime</a>, in the way that he notices things and sees things in a manner that most would not.<br /><br /><br /><br />At one point in the book, Felix sees Nazis burning Jewish books, so he becomes convinced that the Nazis are doing all of this because the Nazis hate the Jews' books and not just them. So in order to make sense of it all, he tells himself a story about how in a book warehouse in another country, one day a bunch of Jewish books fell on top of someone's kids and crushed them. So these people vowed to take revenge on the books and anyone who owns them.<br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>It is truly remarkable the creations that a mind can invent to explain that which it does not know.</strong><br /><br /><br /><br />__________________________________<br /><br /><br /><br />On another note, I have searched high and low on the Internet for another edition of the "Once..." game that I played as a kid and have been entirely unsucessful in finding it.<br /><br /><br /><br />I'm beginning to think that maybe my CIA agent mother had a single copy designed and produced in order to help cultivate my imagination. See, now that doesn't sound at ALL unreasonable to me. But that's just how I roll...</span>
  <a data-text-id="3527758646706333937" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1249329-marjorie-ingall?shelf=middle-grade-to-ya">middle-grade-to-ya</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132992792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9046903072798773991">I did not want to like Once. I hated that cover line: “Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least once.” Damn it, “anyone” does not agree with “their”! Even if I am the last person fighting this battle I will continue to fight it! GAH! But um, more importantly, Once sounded to me like a rehash of The Boy in the Striped Pajamas, a book I loathed. Children (and adults) do not need faux-naif, manipulative, emotionally inauthentic Holocaust books. I’d thought Once was about an</span>
  <span id="freeText9046903072798773991" style="display:none">I did not want to like Once. I hated that cover line: “Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least once.” Damn it, “anyone” does not agree with “their”! Even if I am the last person fighting this battle I will continue to fight it! GAH! But um, more importantly, Once sounded to me like a rehash of The Boy in the Striped Pajamas, a book I loathed. Children (and adults) do not need faux-naif, manipulative, emotionally inauthentic Holocaust books. I’d thought Once was about an equally clueless protagonist. I was wrong. Felix, a Jewish boy in Poland in 1942, isn’t an idiot. He’s in denial. There's a difference. And as the book goes on and the horrors mount, Felix’s denial evaporates. Storytelling has been his shield and survival strategy. As he loses that ability to tell himself truth-deflecting stories, you feel sick. The pacing of this book is incredible – Gleitzman is known in his native Australia for writing funny, goofy contemporary children’s books – and the book’s short paragraphs and use of humor (no, really) will make it super-enticing to boys and non-book-lovers. But be forewarned, the Nazis’ brutality is explicit and disgusting; this should not be any child’s first Holocaust novel. (Start with Number the Stars instead.) Once, which is influenced by the story of Janusz Korczak, offers no false hope. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3021033068469315706">Finding this book in the childrens section of the library, I took it out for my daughter to read. Having glanced at some reviews I thought I would look at it first to see if it were suitable for an 11 yr old.   <span id="freeText3021033068469315706" style="display:none">Finding this book in the childrens section of the library, I took it out for my daughter to read. Having glanced at some reviews I thought I would look at it first to see if it were suitable for an 11 yr old. When I had read a few lines I continued to read the whole book as it was such a compelling story. It was very harrowing and it would not be suitable for younger or sensitive readers. This story about an orphanage during world war 2 has a wonderful main character. Felix is Jewish and living in an orphanage, he runs away to find his parents, he refuses to accept they might not be alive. He invents stories which keep the horrible reality of what's happening around them at arms length. He is wonderfully optimistic and has a lovely personality. I found him very engaging and funny. A really well written book, very sad but also it feels like there is hope for Felix, and if anyone can escape or survive it will be him. Going to see if the library has the next book in the series.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1677132934" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1677132934" />
    <a title="PorshaJo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo"><img alt="PorshaJo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451425289p2/5464046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1677132934?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PorshaJo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PorshaJo" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo">PorshaJo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads">challengereads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challenge">challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads-2016">challengereads-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1677132934" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12744165888527594077">What can you say about a book with a small child living through the Holocaust. The story focuses on Felix, a small 10 year old Jewish boy, who is living in an orphanage. His parents put him in this place a little over three years ago for safety. So he isn't exactly an orphan. Felix is very naive and believes the Nazi's are burning books because they don't like books and sets off to find his parents and save all the books in his parents book store. I was a bit shocked initially how naive he is bu</span>
  <span id="freeText12744165888527594077" style="display:none">What can you say about a book with a small child living through the Holocaust. The story focuses on Felix, a small 10 year old Jewish boy, who is living in an orphanage. His parents put him in this place a little over three years ago for safety. So he isn't exactly an orphan. Felix is very naive and believes the Nazi's are burning books because they don't like books and sets off to find his parents and save all the books in his parents book store. I was a bit shocked initially how naive he is but children were quite different long time ago compared to today. Felix doesn't stay too naive for long after the horrors he endures and witnesses. Its heartbreaking to see Felix realize what is going on around him and what the Nazis are really after.<br /><br />It's not an easy read in terms of the subject matter. However, the author does a great job capturing the story for young children. It is a very short book but the first in a series of following Felix and his friends. I plan to read the remaining in the series, but taking a break in between for a bit of light reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12744165888527594077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_653349194" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/653349194" />
    <a title="Siobhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13213279-siobhan"><img alt="Siobhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1370787077p2/13213279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/653349194?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Siobhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Siobhan" href="/user/show/13213279-siobhan">Siobhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13213279-siobhan?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13213279-siobhan?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13213279-siobhan?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer653349194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8426154234888703695">I remember reading this book as a child still in primary school. It was a project and I was the only child in the class who fully understood what the child in the story was talking about. The burning books, the angry men in uniforms, the train, the carriages, the fear... What he was experiencing. No one else knew what it was that was so scary and terrifying about this story, except me. I guess that makes it worse in the long run.<br/>I think that's why this story has stuck with me into my adulthood. </span>
  <span id="freeText8426154234888703695" style="display:none">I remember reading this book as a child still in primary school. It was a project and I was the only child in the class who fully understood what the child in the story was talking about. The burning books, the angry men in uniforms, the train, the carriages, the fear... What he was experiencing. No one else knew what it was that was so scary and terrifying about this story, except me. I guess that makes it worse in the long run.<br />I think that's why this story has stuck with me into my adulthood. I remember sitting in the library and our classroom and our teacher would read to us, or we would read aloud to each other. I remember it so vividly and sometimes I believe I can go back to that place, in that beanbag or chair and just relive those stories with my teachers because they didn't shy away from the confronting issues. Together, though we were children, we faced them head on.<br />That's why this books was one of my favourites. The sheer scale and the terror, but also the hope. Because even in the darkest night there is still a shimmer of light.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8426154234888703695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paloma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paloma" href="/user/show/32176884-paloma">Paloma</a>

I re-read it in grade 5 and still loved it but the second time I read it, I looked deeper into details, because I already knew what was going to happen, so I payed attention to all of the magnificent details Morris Gleitzman added to the story. I strongly recommend anyone reading this, it's a great book and I hope you enjoy it :)</span>

<div id="review_322637319" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/322637319" />
    <a title="Alex  Baugh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4449236-alex-baugh"><img alt="Alex  Baugh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439208555p2/4449236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/322637319?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex  Baugh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex  Baugh" href="/user/show/4449236-alex-baugh">Alex  Baugh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780805090260" href="/book/show/7061392-once">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4449236-alex-baugh?shelf=world-war-2">world-war-2</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer322637319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9113907669038242002">Once I read a story about a 10 year old Jewish boy named Felix who lived in Poland in 1942 and I felt a terrible sadness as I read.<br/><br/>Once is very poignantly narrated by Felix. He tells the reader that he had been placed in a Catholic orphanage by his parents, booksellers in Poland, and has lived there for three years. He also tells the reader that he likes to make up stories and is considered by others to be quite good at it. Felix always carries a notebook in which he writes down his stories and </span>
  <span id="freeText9113907669038242002" style="display:none">Once I read a story about a 10 year old Jewish boy named Felix who lived in Poland in 1942 and I felt a terrible sadness as I read.<br /><br />Once is very poignantly narrated by Felix. He tells the reader that he had been placed in a Catholic orphanage by his parents, booksellers in Poland, and has lived there for three years. He also tells the reader that he likes to make up stories and is considered by others to be quite good at it. Felix always carries a notebook in which he writes down his stories and hides it inside his shirt for safekeeping. Once he found a whole carrot in his soup and told himself that it was a sign that his Mum and Dad were coming back to get him. But they never show up. When Nazis suddenly show up at the orphanage to burn the books in the library, Felix remembers the time they destroyed the books in his parent’s bookshop and reasons with himself that the Nazis are only after books. He decides to leave the orphanage and find his Mum and Dad, to warn them that their books are in danger. <br /><br />As Felix goes along looking for his parents, he starts to tell himself stories about what he sees. For example, a few days after he begins his journey, Felix comes across an empty house, with the kitchen stove still burning and half eaten meals on the table. He tells himself a story about the people who live there and why they have left so suddenly: <br />In the distance I hear faint gunshots. <br />Of course. That explains it. They’re out hunting. They must have seen some rabbits, grabbed their guns and gone after them in a big hurry. (pg33)<br />The reader of course knows why the house is empty and what the gunshots are even if Felix doesn’t. <br /><br />Felix is a clever boy who not only creates his own stories, he also uses the knowledge he has acquired from other stories he is familiar with. As his search for his Mum and Dad takes Felix towards an unnamed city, he sees a house on fire and the bloody owners lying outside on the ground. Not sure if they are dead or alive, he picks up a feather and holds it in front of their noses: “It is how you tell if people are dead. I read it in a book once.” (pg.55) This is also the house where he finds six year old Zelda and the place where a very scary suspicion of reality begins to occur to Felix:<br />A little kid. What sort of people would kill a little kid just for the sake of some books? A horrible thought grows in my throbbing head. What if us Jews aren’t being bullied just because of books? What if it’s because of something else? (pg 56)<br />Felix continues his journey now accompanied by the traumatized Zelda, to whom he continuously tells stories. As they continue on towards the unnamed city, they run into a large group of weary men, women and children walking along the road, all wearing armbands with Jewish stars, and being escorted by Nazi soldiers. At first, Felix thinks they are all Jewish book owners. Forced by the Nazis to join the group, another bit of reality scarily intrudes into Felix’s thoughts after asking one of the men if he is a book lover, then wishes he hadn’t: <br />Not just because I’ve made a suffering Jewish man feel upset at the sight of a crazy kid. Also because I’ve got a horrible suspicion I know the answer to the question.<br />Maybe it’s not just our books the Nazis hate. <br />Maybe it’s us.<br />When Felix and Zelda finally arrive in the city, they are taken into hiding by a dentist who is already taking care of several other children whose parents have been put on transport trains. The dentist, Barney, is a character modeled after a man who actually did take care of Jewish orphans. Janusz Korczak was a Jewish children’s doctor and well known author. When the Nazis rounded up the orphans he was caring for to transport them to Treblinka, they offer Korczak sanctuary. He refused to leave the children, choosing instead to go to his death with them. In his Afterword to Once, Gleitzman explains that Korczak became his hero after reading about him. <br /><br />Once is the story Felix tells us about his life as a child in this particular time and place in the history of the Holocaust. His journey to find his Mum and Dad also turns out to be Felix’s journey from innocence to realization about the situation of Jews under the Nazis. The book is geared for readers aged 11 and up and I would highly recommend it for that age group. Younger readers, even those who have high reading levels, may still find it too disturbing. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9113907669038242002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97699475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3296158930117755326">This is an all in one sitting type of read, and I did read it all in one day. It’s told from the point of view of a 9-10 year old Jewish boy, a storyteller, who’s caught up in the Holocaust. This story is powerful, compelling, and so very sad, yet somehow uplifting too, and even amusing every once in a while. I’ll be thinking quite a bit about Felix, Zelda, Barney, and some other characters. Felix is a fine narrator and everyone and everything come across very vividly. The author’s note at the e</span>
  <span id="freeText3296158930117755326" style="display:none">This is an all in one sitting type of read, and I did read it all in one day. It’s told from the point of view of a 9-10 year old Jewish boy, a storyteller, who’s caught up in the Holocaust. This story is powerful, compelling, and so very sad, yet somehow uplifting too, and even amusing every once in a while. I’ll be thinking quite a bit about Felix, Zelda, Barney, and some other characters. Felix is a fine narrator and everyone and everything come across very vividly. The author’s note at the end is wonderful. My favorite line in it is “This story is my imagination trying to grasp the unimaginable.” I’m off to check out his website: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.morrisgleitzman.com">www.morrisgleitzman.com</a>. This book is book 1 of a trilogy and I’ve already reserved book 2 at the library. This book is in the children’s section of my library, but I’d say it’s best for older children, teens, and adults because it doesn’t at all shy away from the atrocities, and even though Felix starts off innocent and ignorant, he’s certainly not able to stay that way. There is one twist in the plot about one of the characters that I didn’t see coming, and that I thought was brilliant to include. Highly recommended, but not for kids who don’t already know about the Holocaust.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3296158930117755326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_169238436" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169238436?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" href="/user/show/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer">Krista the Krazy Kataloguer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-holocaust">read-holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-survival">read-survival</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-historical-fiction">read-historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-ya">read-ya</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169238436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7215798750868484502">Now, I would have classed this short book as J rather than YA had there not been some horrifying scenes in it. The narrator, Felix, is a young Jewish boy who has been living in an orphanage in 1942 Poland, where his parents left him and from which he expects them to fetch him some day. What makes this such a chilling and hard-to-put-down book is Felix's naive and innocent view of the horrific events around him. It reminded me of John Boyne's <i>The Boy in the Striped Pajamas</i> in that respect. As the</span>
  <span id="freeText7215798750868484502" style="display:none">Now, I would have classed this short book as J rather than YA had there not been some horrifying scenes in it. The narrator, Felix, is a young Jewish boy who has been living in an orphanage in 1942 Poland, where his parents left him and from which he expects them to fetch him some day. What makes this such a chilling and hard-to-put-down book is Felix's naive and innocent view of the horrific events around him. It reminded me of John Boyne's <i>The Boy in the Striped Pajamas</i> in that respect. As the reader, I understood the true significance of events he was seeing and misinterpreting, and at every chapter I kept expecting his naivete to get him killed or captured by the Nazis. Fortunately, Felix slowly begins to understand the true nature of events around him, yet he continues to cling to the idea that his parents are alive. This book had me literally tense with suspense. I wanted to put it down to relieve the tension, but I just had to read on. The story ended abruptly, begging for the sequel I know has been written and which I absolutely must read. This is a truly scary story, not for the faint of heart. Recommended for teens and adults.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7215798750868484502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/49013147-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49013147-david?shelf=holocaust">holocaust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/49013147-david?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1812732773" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer243499841364266291">3.5 Holocaust books are always so hard to rate because regardless of the quality of the book I commend the author for trying to tell such an important and difficult story. This story takes the form of the perspective of a nine year old Polish boy trying to understand the horror of his circumstances. I think the makings of an excellent book were definitely here, and that the author did a commendable job, but it just didn't work for me. Having a nine year old at home certainly allowed me to really</span>
  <span id="freeText243499841364266291" style="display:none">3.5 Holocaust books are always so hard to rate because regardless of the quality of the book I commend the author for trying to tell such an important and difficult story. This story takes the form of the perspective of a nine year old Polish boy trying to understand the horror of his circumstances. I think the makings of an excellent book were definitely here, and that the author did a commendable job, but it just didn't work for me. Having a nine year old at home certainly allowed me to really think about the book from that perspective, but I just felt like that perspective didn't come off in an authentic and accurate way. I am very happy I read this book because although it is heartbreaking, it did make me contemplate one of history's worst atrocities through the eyes of children. This is a lesson that is good to remember when one looks at history, or even makes decisions in the present. My knit picking aside, I do think this is a book that a lot of people would really enjoy and that is accessible to numerous reading levels and age groups.</span>

<div id="review_1939397536" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1939397536" />
    <a title="Claire (Book Blog Bird)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10982678-claire-book-blog-bird"><img alt="Claire (Book Blog Bird)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343948552p2/10982678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1939397536?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Claire (Book Blog Bird)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Claire (Book Blog Bird)" href="/user/show/10982678-claire-book-blog-bird">Claire (Book Blog Bird)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1939397536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9003206690100967910"><b>Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least Once.</b><br/><br/>Once is the heartbreaking, harrowing but ultimately hope-filled story of Felix, a ten year old Polish Jewish boy living through the Nazi occupation of Poland and the Holocaust. He lives in an orphanage in the Polish mountains - his parents, who are booksellers, left him there when they went off to solve all sorts of book-related emergencies (according to Felix).<br/><br/>Life in the orphanage sounds pretty sad and desperate - the condi</span>
  <span id="freeText9003206690100967910" style="display:none"><b>Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least Once.</b><br /><br />Once is the heartbreaking, harrowing but ultimately hope-filled story of Felix, a ten year old Polish Jewish boy living through the Nazi occupation of Poland and the Holocaust. He lives in an orphanage in the Polish mountains - his parents, who are booksellers, left him there when they went off to solve all sorts of book-related emergencies (according to Felix).<br /><br />Life in the orphanage sounds pretty sad and desperate - the conditions are pretty basic, there's notmuch food and thenunsare pretty strict - but very gradually you learn the truth - that the nuns doing all they can to keep the Nazis away from the orphanage so keep the children (including a number of Jewish children) safe.<br /><br />Gradually, Felix comes to realise the truth about the Holocaust and the Nazis and what is happening to the Jews in Poland (spoiler alert: nothing nice). We're shown some of the atrocities of the Holocaust through the eyes of a ten year old child which somehow makes them all the more horrifying.<br /><br />One of the most heartbreaking things I found about the story (apart from the whole Holocaust thing, which obviously gets top spot) is the way Felix makes up stories in his head as a way of trying to explain away the awful things that are happening around him. It's just so sad. And his horror and bravery when he realises what's going on - you really forget that this is just a little kid.<br /><br />Even though there have been a lot of stories written about the Holocaust, this book ought to be required reading. It was one of the biggest (if not the biggest) atrocities of the twentieth century and I don't think it's the kind of subject that will ever be 'done with'. And that's the whole point. As long as we keep reminding ourselves about what happened, then maybe we stand a chance of not repeating our mistakes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9003206690100967910" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17944731111392918682" style="display:none">I love this series. At first, I hated how this book was so childish and disgusting, it made me feel stupid that Ms. Burns recommended it to me. I'm now on the 4th book of the series. Now that I look back, this book makes me feel like I've experienced a holocaust survivor child's suffering and lifetime. I feel like a proud mother seeing them grow old but at the same time their best friend. This book made me feel the loss and sadness that the main character felt (but I would also never in my life compare my emotions to an orphan child in the holocaust). The style of writing is just... oh my god.... I CRY THINKING ABOUT IT. It's too good. The voice. Thinking about how good this book is, is like dude....... Felix's narration is so comical and noodle-heady but at the same time, you can see the maturity as time goes on. Read this series. READ IT. I KNOW YOU WANT TO. READ IT NOW, THIS IS A THREAT.<br /><br />and to Morris Gleitzman, I wonder how it feels to write a holocaust children's book and then accidentally create a religion. Once is my ~religion~</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16959660476183760247">Felix's naivety is almost unbelievable at times. But then you think of the trauma a kid must go through, and how he was VERY misinformed by his parents- add that up with the number of deaths he sees, and you can sorta understand what the author was getting at. So here are some things I've learned from reading Holocaust books:<br/><br/>1. Being a Jew must suck. They got chased out of Israel by the Romans, which was when the fled to Europe, which was where many perished at the hands of the Nazis, which was </span>
  <span id="freeText16959660476183760247" style="display:none">Felix's naivety is almost unbelievable at times. But then you think of the trauma a kid must go through, and how he was VERY misinformed by his parents- add that up with the number of deaths he sees, and you can sorta understand what the author was getting at. So here are some things I've learned from reading Holocaust books:<br /><br />1. Being a Jew must suck. They got chased out of Israel by the Romans, which was when the fled to Europe, which was where many perished at the hands of the Nazis, which was why they fled BACK to Israel, which was why the Palestinians are so pissed (because the Jews took THEIR land of course) which is why there were like a billion wars that followed (Six Day War, Arab-Israeli War, Yom Kippur War, etc.) Which is why now the Jews have their own country that is the only Jewish country in the entire world that is completely surrounded by Arab nations laden with Palestinian refugees who all want Israel dead. So yeah, you would probably suffer more if you were a Jew.<br /><br />2. Being German must suck. They probably still get mouthfuls of soap for what they did during WW2<br /><br />3. No matter what the story, every Holocaust book has death, pain, anguish, suffering, death, pain, anguish, and suffering. Which, if you think about it, is pretty good. No stuff is glossed over and you KNOW that it's happened. Which makes the story even more depressing, since it's based during an actual event in history instead of, say, the trolls fighting the fairies over who gets the unicorn.<br /><br />So, yes I WAS depressed after reading Once, because of all of the above, but it was actually a pretty neat story. I finished it in maybe an hour so it definitely is a pretty quick read. <br /><br />Believe it or not, one of my favorite type of books are the Holocaust type. Anytime you need a slice of reality, just pick up the Boy in the Striped Pj's, or the Diary of Anne Frank. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16959660476183760247" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2801463018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2801463018" />
    <a title="Lorraine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5154754-lorraine"><img alt="Lorraine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1300849667p2/5154754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2801463018?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lorraine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lorraine" href="/user/show/5154754-lorraine">Lorraine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2801463018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3122391678705063850">Once is the story of 10 year old Felix, a Jewish boy living in a Catholic orphanage during WWII. His parents are booksellers and Felix believes that they are out searching for ways to help their business. He doesn't realize that they are dead, killed by the Nazi's and not coming back. Felix has a wild imagination and is always making up stories. So when he receives a whole carrot in his soup, he takes it as a sign that his parents are still alive and coming back for him. When they don't come, he</span>
  <span id="freeText3122391678705063850" style="display:none">Once is the story of 10 year old Felix, a Jewish boy living in a Catholic orphanage during WWII. His parents are booksellers and Felix believes that they are out searching for ways to help their business. He doesn't realize that they are dead, killed by the Nazi's and not coming back. Felix has a wild imagination and is always making up stories. So when he receives a whole carrot in his soup, he takes it as a sign that his parents are still alive and coming back for him. When they don't come, he decides to leave the orphanage and go search for them himself.<br /><br />Having no idea of the atrocities that the Jews are enduring, he sets out across Poland looking for them. Along the way, he encounters many clues such as empty houses, gunshots in the distance, and a different family living in his house, and he slowly begins to piece together how bad things have gotten. When he rescues Zelda, a young girl whose parents have been shot and killed, he uses the art of storytelling to avoid telling her the truth about her parents. Eventually the two are captured and sent to live a ghetto. Felix has to accept the facts of what is going on around him and slowly realizes that his own parents are also not coming back.<br /><br />This book has some very sad parts. I think it is a good choice for students as an introduction to Holocaust. Felix, is very naive in the beginning of the story. He invents stories which keep the horrible reality of what's happening around him from becoming a reality. As the reality dawns on his, he doesn't give up hope. He is optimistic and cares for others around him. This is a really well written book. The reader is rooting for Felix to survive. I highly recommend it and can't wait to read others in the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3122391678705063850" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_708413938" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/708413938" />
    <a title="Jackie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1176067-jackie"><img alt="Jackie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518141143p2/1176067.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/708413938?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackie" href="/user/show/1176067-jackie">Jackie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781740949026" href="/book/show/17795679-once">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1176067-jackie?shelf=2013-read">2013-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1176067-jackie?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer708413938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13651007672798298158">I came across this one through <a href='http://www.audiobooksync.com/sync-young-adult-titles/' rel='nofollow noopener'>YA Sync’s free audio summer program.</a> Generally, I’m not into war stories, particularly those written from a child’s perspective. I just don’t have a good track record with them. I’ve read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/69392.Milkweed' title='Milkweed by Jerry Spinelli' rel='nofollow noopener'>Milkweed</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39999.The_Boy_in_the_Striped_Pajamas' title='The Boy in the Striped Pajamas by John Boyne' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Boy in the Striped Pajamas</a>, both of which are similar to Once. In both books, I wanted to slap the narrators for their naivety of their situations. Since this one was free and the audio was only 3 hours I decided to give it a try.<br/><br/>The story is told from Felix’s per</span>
  <span id="freeText13651007672798298158" style="display:none">I came across this one through <a href="http://www.audiobooksync.com/sync-young-adult-titles/" rel="nofollow noopener">YA Sync’s free audio summer program.</a> Generally, I’m not into war stories, particularly those written from a child’s perspective. I just don’t have a good track record with them. I’ve read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/69392.Milkweed" title="Milkweed by Jerry Spinelli" rel="nofollow noopener">Milkweed</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/39999.The_Boy_in_the_Striped_Pajamas" title="The Boy in the Striped Pajamas by John Boyne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Boy in the Striped Pajamas</a>, both of which are similar to Once. In both books, I wanted to slap the narrators for their naivety of their situations. Since this one was free and the audio was only 3 hours I decided to give it a try.<br /><br />The story is told from Felix’s perspective, a young Jewish boy, who was left by his parents in a Catholic orphanage. His parents were booksellers, which explains Felix’s love of books and storytelling. Because Felix likes to tell stories he is not the most reliable narrator. Many times he makes up stories to explain away the horrors he encounters or to simply make himself feel better. While, I did sometimes want to slap Felix for his naivety I did give him a pass because of his bookish background. Actually, I think that is why Once’s narration worked for me better than the other two books did. <br /><br />I ended up liking Felix quite a bit. I found myself sympathizing with him and wanting to protect him. Of course war is horrific, but reading it from the perspective of a child seems to be more heart wrenching. I thought the plot and perspective were well done and at no point did I feel emotionally manipulated. <br /><br />Apart from Felix there are a few other characters important to the story, like Zelda and Barney. Zelda reminded me of Ygritte from “A Games of Thrones” series with her constant “Don’t you know anything?”. After a third time or so of listening to it I was ready to throttle her. Barney’s character was definitely interesting and I was surprised to find out that his character was based on a true story. This made the ending more poignant for me. <br /><br />Overall, despite some minor complaints this one was enjoyable as a war time read could be. The book is marked for middle grade kids, but just be warned that there is death and a bit of violence in the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13651007672798298158" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_614416584" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/614416584" />
    <a title="Susan Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8245824-susan-rose"><img alt="Susan Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483966357p2/8245824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/614416584?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Rose" href="/user/show/8245824-susan-rose">Susan Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer614416584" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14375122652974078373">This is a holocaust book which is narrated by a child, I was hoping this book wasn’t going to be like The Boy in the Striped Pajamas….. I was disappointed. <br/><br/>(This is not a spoiler, it’s set up in the first few pages). The premise of this book is that a Jewish child doesn’t know about the holocaust as it is happening because he has been shielded by the Catholic nuns who are looking after him in a Polish orphanage which his parents have hidden him in. Not only does the boy (Felix) not know about th</span>
  <span id="freeText14375122652974078373" style="display:none">This is a holocaust book which is narrated by a child, I was hoping this book wasn’t going to be like The Boy in the Striped Pajamas….. I was disappointed. <br /><br />(This is not a spoiler, it’s set up in the first few pages). The premise of this book is that a Jewish child doesn’t know about the holocaust as it is happening because he has been shielded by the Catholic nuns who are looking after him in a Polish orphanage which his parents have hidden him in. Not only does the boy (Felix) not know about the persecutions of Jews he also doesn’t know about Nazi’s, or the German language (he doesn’t even know that’s what they’re called), but he does know the name Adolf Hitler but not who he is. This all felt ridiculous even in a fairly isolated school in occupied Poland there would have been Nazi involvement, lessons and curriculum. <br /><br />All in all this book frustrated me in the same way that The Boy in the Striped Pajamas frustrated me, it is overly simplistic to the point of patronising and historically inaccurate. I will say however that this book isn’t as bad as The Boy in the Striped Pajamas children who read this book will learn more about the holocaust and there is a lot more character development, which makes it much more moving. (I would suggest kids will learn even more by reading Anne Frank’s Diary, but there we go).<br /><br />The further the book goes on the more historically accurate and believable the story and characters become but it is quite hard to forget the rather silly beginning. <br /><br />Basically I wouldn’t recommend it to adult or YA readers, I think if you have even a vague understanding of WWII and the Holocaust this book will be too simplistic historically for you and I don’t think this book is written in a particularly beautiful or original style to make up for that.<br /><br />I can sum up my feeling of this book in this closing sentence: don’t read this read The Book Thief instead. <br /><br />Rating 2.5-3</span>
  <a data-text-id="14375122652974078373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1895461694" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1895461694" />
    <a title="~Bellegirl91~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3893565-bellegirl91"><img alt="~Bellegirl91~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507600606p2/3893565.jpg" /></a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1895461694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13555350818983361856">The theme of this book seems to be "EVERYBODY DESERVES TO HAVE SOMETHING GOOD IN THEIR LIFE. AT LEAST ONCE." Felix is a naive 10 year old little Jewish boy who had to pose as a Catholic boy in an orphanage and was being hidden by a Mother superior I believe, named Mother Minka who was a regular customer of Felix's family Bookshop. So for "three years and eight months" he's been in this orphanage and NEVER EVER told about what may have truly happened to his parents, why they had hidden him, nor A</span>
  <span id="freeText13555350818983361856" style="display:none">The theme of this book seems to be "EVERYBODY DESERVES TO HAVE SOMETHING GOOD IN THEIR LIFE. AT LEAST ONCE." Felix is a naive 10 year old little Jewish boy who had to pose as a Catholic boy in an orphanage and was being hidden by a Mother superior I believe, named Mother Minka who was a regular customer of Felix's family Bookshop. So for "three years and eight months" he's been in this orphanage and NEVER EVER told about what may have truly happened to his parents, why they had hidden him, nor ANYTHING about the outside world.<br /><br /><br /><br />Until one day Nazis come to the Orphanage and burns A TON of books in a bonfire in the courtyard. So poor little Felix, who has no idea about the true nature of Nazis and he thinks they're going about burning books. So when he decides to escape the Orphanage in search of his parents to make sure their bookshop is safe from "Book burning Nazis" as he called them, he's in for the surprise of a lifetime. Especially when he sees an abandoned home with gunshots and the home had a Jewish flag outside the house, and saw a breakfast half eaten and still fresh. Assuming the family has left for hunting because they most likely saw rabbit or something and quickly left their breakfast to go hunt them for supper/dinner.<br /><br /><br /><br />Well, at some point in his travels home which was at the bottom of the mountain from the Orphanage, he was walking down a dirt road when a vehicle came by him and a Nazi shot near his feet leading Felix to jump away. He thought the Nazi was just playing a game and didn't think the soldier was serious however. But these vehicles had people with and without clothes being transported somewhere and also in nine year old little Felix's naive desensitized mind of the truth of it all, he keeps on walking when the vehicles and the Nazi that shot at him (and missed) disappeared, he hears more gun fire close by and is by a river but he can't see the direction it's coming from.<br /><br /><br /><br /> "Boy, gunfire's loud when it's so close. That lot startled me so much <br /> I almost fell into the water. The hunters must be just round the bend<br /> in the river. Another burst of gunfire, a long one. And another.<br /> ....Look at that. The river suddenly turned red. Which is a bit strange, <br /> because the sunset is still yellow. The water's so red it almost looks like<br /> blood. But even with all those gunshots, the hunters couldn't have<br /> killed that many rabbits. Could they? No, it must just be a trick of the light."<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />*heart shattering* And once he found his home and family bookshop lost and overtaken by a non-Jewish family he once knew and treated him quite opposite with so much cruelty and telling him they were going to turn him in, from then on does his journey begin and soon after much later does he at some point truly find out the truth very slowly of the truth that the Nazis aren't actually "book burning Nazis" but that they hate his people. <br /><br /><br /><br />There's MORE to this book than meets just that. It's well thought out, it's fascinating, yes a quick read but it's so worth it and so original. But Morris Gleitzman was able to use WWII Holocaust facts and put it into an unforgettable and un-put-downable book. This started out by me reading it to a neighbor's 91 year old mom who has partial dementia (her mom's day aides can't be there everyday at her house and needs a few extra hands to help out) by reading this out loud to her and I couldn't help but just finish it after a while of not going up as often for a few weeks and at the same time I'm glad I did.<br /><br /><br /><br />I remember reading this one time and noticed the last time I read it was 2013 and the ONLY reason I now own the books is because my county library doesn't have them all and plus I thought it'd be fun to own them. Later I found out there was a 6th book in the series while looking here on goodreads and I was so ecstatic and then to find out there's a soon 7th and final book? Now I'm even MORE excited for it after reading this one.<br /><br /><br /><br />so to end it, there was one thing that broke my heart even more when Felix was leaving his home village and came across two children playing a game. (this was BEFORE he met a young girl named Zelda and one day would learn the full truth of what's really going on. and later it's revealed it's the year 1942).....<br /><br /><br /><br /> "I turn around. Two little kids are staring at me, a boy and a girl,<br /> barefoot in the dust.<br /> 'We're playing grabbing Jews in the street.' says the little boy.<br /> 'I'm a Jew,' says the little girl. 'He's a Nazi. He's going to grab me<br /> and take me away. Who do you want to be?'<br /> I don't say anything.<br /> 'You be a Nazi,' says the little girl, squinting at me in the sunlight.<br /> I shake my head.<br /> 'Alright, you be a Jew," she says. 'That means you have to be sad cause<br /> the Nazis took your mum and dad away.'<br /> I stare at her. She give an impatient sigh.<br /> 'All the Jew people got taken,' she says. 'My dad told me. So you have <br /> to be sad, alright?'<br /> Relax, I tell myself. It's just a game. But panic is churning inside me.<br /> 'He doesn't want to play,' says the little boy.<br /> The little boy is right, I don't."<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />So overall this book is just heartbreaking with moments like this, as I've mentioned before there's MORE to this story than just mentioned here and now I can't wait to get to the next book THEN. So to end this officially, no matter what ANYONE or ANY OF YOU may be going through in your life and when bad things happen, know there's some good in it. Even if it's small. Because "Everybody deserves to have something good in their life. At least ONCE."<br /><br /><br /><br />***solid SOLID 5+ stars***</span>
  <a data-text-id="13555350818983361856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18417351168675400661">I was uncomfortable with the protagonist's view of the holocaust. I appreciated the self-denial aspect, but I also thought a child of that age group would've been more intelligent. The failure to comprehend (or admit comprehension) carried on far too long in my view. <br/>I know the rest of the world adored this book. It is well-written and has some quirky, everyday touches viz. the way Spielberg placed the red jacket in his largely B&W film, "Schindler's List". <br/>But quite frankly I thought this book </span>
  <span id="freeText18417351168675400661" style="display:none">I was uncomfortable with the protagonist's view of the holocaust. I appreciated the self-denial aspect, but I also thought a child of that age group would've been more intelligent. The failure to comprehend (or admit comprehension) carried on far too long in my view. <br />I know the rest of the world adored this book. It is well-written and has some quirky, everyday touches viz. the way Spielberg placed the red jacket in his largely B&amp;W film, "Schindler's List". <br />But quite frankly I thought this book diminished the child reader's acuity and as such was not (for me) helpful with the overall topic itself. <br /><br />I am reminded that this is a very sensitive area within a genre needing care &amp; consideration. I admire the bravery of those who go there for the sake of young readers. I hope I haven't offended anyone who read and admired this book, but to me it is not a children's book because of the content &amp; it is not an adult's book because of the prose. Sorry.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1274852332041411036">I don't remember much about this book, except that it was about the Holocaust and I was in middle school, the target audience. I remember a train, a girl named Zelda, and I remember feeling sad and horrified but never nightmares. Most of all, I remember this story was a great way for an 11-year-old me to experience the Holocaust for the first time. It didn't water down a single thing, but it also used the perspective of a child EXTRAORDINARILY well to hide some of the true horrors behind the inn</span>
  <span id="freeText1274852332041411036" style="display:none">I don't remember much about this book, except that it was about the Holocaust and I was in middle school, the target audience. I remember a train, a girl named Zelda, and I remember feeling sad and horrified but never nightmares. Most of all, I remember this story was a great way for an 11-year-old me to experience the Holocaust for the first time. It didn't water down a single thing, but it also used the perspective of a child EXTRAORDINARILY well to hide some of the true horrors behind the innocence of the characters. I fervently believe an adult could read this and feel just the same as a child because of expertly it was crafted for that specific reason. Full endorsement.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9981605007451342953">A beautiful and tragic Holocaust story, told through the innocent eyes of a nine-year-old boy. Sheltered in a Polish orphanage, posing as a Catholic, he has no idea what's going on around him until he runs away to find his parents. What he witnesses he at first does not understand, but the reader does and gradually Felix's naiteve is stripped away. If you like Jerry Spinelli's <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/69392.Milkweed' title='Milkweed by Jerry Spinelli' rel='nofollow noopener'>Milkweed</a>, you'll love <i>Once</i>.</span>

Sheltered in a Polish orphanage, posing as a Catholic, he has no idea what's going on around him until he runs away to find his parents. What he witnesses he at first does not understand, but the reader does and gradually Felix's naiteve is stripped away. If you like Jerry Spinelli's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/69392.Milkweed" title="Milkweed by Jerry Spinelli" rel="nofollow noopener">Milkweed</a>, you'll love <i>Once</i>.</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80638935-hp-saucerer?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80638935-hp-saucerer?shelf=grief">grief</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80638935-hp-saucerer?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80638935-hp-saucerer?shelf=world-war-ii">world-war-ii</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80638935-hp-saucerer?shelf=first-person-narrative">first-person-narrative</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/80638935-hp-saucerer?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2398417042" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15303179172578805783">Once is a remarkable story. Despite the evident terrors in the book, there is also a great deal of humour and warmth. Gleitzman has you smiling at one paragraph and grimacing at the next. An equally breathtaking and yet heartbreaking story, that portrays the suffering and misery of the Holocaust with grace and sincerity.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

Its such a heart warming and heart breaking story to read. I don't want to give anything away but as a short book I would recommend this to young readers interested in learning about the holocaust and what young Jewish kids as well as the wider Jewish and ethnic minorities communities went through during this horrific time. The story flows so well its </span>
  <span id="freeText5659718982789543258" style="display:none">This is the story of a young boy in nazi occupied Poland during WW2. The story flows so well its easily read in one sitting.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8jRrtPNnglEJ3L9MQgwoSUVphWzI11sS/FNutp9zPs1Vey+qHwXxPl+8SV318dy7/u2dL+L7ePALE6pjU2Bidw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('S51qOB5oklljCnmulfO1cpBMW/HAtRB4cmxcCSUK/3rs0i4m8grhNjVqj78iDkGAK8hDsuqZM5qFLJjc6RmjwA==')})
  }
